Priority by Essence vs. Priority by Concept in Definitions
Commentator defends author's order of presenting tasdiq before tasawwur in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between priority by essence (dhat) and priority by concept (mafhum) in definitions and determine which is relevant for a given definitional context.

Core Idea
Definitions are about concepts, not existential priority.

In Islamic logic, there is a subtle distinction between saying something is 'prior' in existence vs. 'prior' in conception. The commentator here addresses an objector who thinks the author should have defined tasawwur before tasdiq. The commentary clarifies that when defining, we deal with concepts, not the actual existence of things. This lesson will walk you through that distinction and its implications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says the author should have presented tasawwur (conception) before tasdiq (assent) in the definition. Note the assumption behind this: that tasawwur is always prior to tasdiq in every respect.
The objector conflates existential priority with conceptual priority.
2
Understand the commentator's two-part response
The commentator distinguishes two meanings of 'prior': priority by essence (individuals/existence) and priority by concept (universal meaning). If the objector means existential priority, it's granted but irrelevant. If conceptual priority, it's denied.
This is a conditional reasoning: If P then Q; if R then not-Q.
3
Apply the distinction to the definitional context
The commentator states that the definition here is 'by concept' (bi hasab al-mafhum), not 'by essence'. Therefore, the appropriate order in definition is determined by logical dependency of concepts, not temporal or existential order.
This is a key principle for all definitions in Islamic sciences.
4
Extend to divisions (taqsim)
The commentator further argues that divisions also consider concepts rather than essences. He cites authorities to show that the divided (muqassam) is the concept, not the essence. This reinforces that the author's order is correct.
This prevents misunderstandings about the role of essence in logical divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ على قُلْتُ: إِنْ عَنَيْتَ بتقديم التَّصورِ على التصديقِ أَنَّ ذاتَه مُتَقَدِّمةٌ على التّصديقِ؛ فمُسَلَّمٌ، لكنَّه غيرُ مُفيدٍ؛ لأنَّ تقديم التصديقِ هَهُنا في التّعريفِ، الدسوقي: واعلَمْ أنَّ المراد بالوضع في قولهم: يقدَّمُ التَّصور على التصديق في الوضع، والذكر، والكتابة، والتعلم، والتعليم. (قَوْلُهُ: إِنْ عَنَيْتَ أي: قصدْتَ بِقولِكَ: التَّصَوُّرُ مُقدَّم على التصديق). (قَوْلُهُ: أَنَّ ذَاتَهُ) أي: أنَّ أفرادَهُ مُقدَّمةٌ على أفرادِ التصديق، وقوله: (فَمُسلَّمْ)؛ أي: فَمُسلَّمْ أَنَّ التَّصور بحسب ذاتِهِ مُقدَّم على التصديق بحسب ذاتِهِ، فَالمراد بالذَّاتِ الأفراد، ويصحُ أنْ يُراد بذاتِهِ نَفْسُهُ؛ أَي: أَنَّ نَفْسَهُ مُقدَّمةُ على نَفْسِ التَّصديقِ في الوجود؛ أي: إن عنيتَ أنَّ وجودَه مُتقدِّمُ على وجودِ التصديق؛ فَمُسلَّم . (قَوْلُهُ: لَكِنَّهُ) أي: التَّقديمُ المذكور، وهو تقديمُ التَّصورِ على التصديق بحسب الذات. (قَوْلُهُ: غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ) أي: لا يفيدُ السَّائلَ؛ أي: المعترض بأنَّ الأَوْلى للمصنِّفِ أن يقدِّمَ التَّصرُّرَ على التصديق . (قَوْلُهُ: لِأَنَّ تَقْدِيمَ التَّصْدِيقِ هَهُنَا فِي التَّعْرِيْفِ أي: تعريف التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ الضّمني الَّذِي تضمَّنَهُ التَّقسيم . العطار: على الإنكار؛ فهو ذاهل عن مصطلح النُّفَّارِ؛ إذ التقاسيم كالتعاريف؛ لا تمنع، وإنما يتكلم عليها بطريق الإبطال كما بينَ في محله (قَوْلُهُ: لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ) أي: فالقياسُ المذكورُ مُسلَّم؛ لكنَّ نتيجتَهُ لا تُخالِفُ الغرض؛ لأنَّهُ إِنَّما أنتج أنَّ ذاتَ التَّصورِ؛ أي: أفراده وماصدقاته متقدّمة على التصديق، وليس الكلامُ فيه، وقولُهُ بَعْدُ: (وإنْ عنيتَ بِهِ أَنَّ مَفهومه . . . إلخ)؛ أي: فالقياس غير تام بمنع الصُّغرى؛ أي: لا نُسلّمُ أَنَّ مَفهومَ التَّصورِ مقدَّمٌ على مفهومِ التصديقِ، والكلامُ هُنَا مفهومه لا أفراده . بحسب (قَوْلُهُ: لِأَنَّ تَقْدِيمَ التَّصْدِيقِ هُنَا) أي: في التَّعريفِ الَّذِي تَضمَّنَهُ التَّقسيم، وقولُ الشَّارحِ: ولَمَّا كانَ بيان الحاجة ... إلخ؛ لا يُنافي كونَ التَّعريف مقصوداً أيضاً  عة المنطقية والتَّعريفُ ليس بحسَب الذَّاتِ، بل بحسب المفهوم. وإِنْ عَنَيْتَ به أنَّ مفهومه مُقدَّمٌ على مفهوم التصديق؛ فممنوع؛ الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: وَالتَّعْرِيْفُ لَيْسَ بِحَسَبِ الذَّاتِ؛ بَلْ بِحَسَبِ الْمَنْهُوم المراد بالمفهوم: وهو المعنى الكُلّي الذي هو إذعانُ النِّسبة الخبريَّةِ بالنسبة مَا يُفهم من اللفظ، للتصديق، وعدم إذعانها بالنسبةِ للتَّصوُّرِ، وحيثُ كانَ التَّعريفُ بحسب المفهوم؛ فالمناسب ما ارتكبه المصنِّفُ من تقديم التّصديق على التّصور . العطار: كالتَّقسيم؛ لأنَّهُ إِنَّما بَيَّنَ جهةً قصدِ التَّقسيم؛ بأنَّ بيان الحاجة يتوقف عليه، وبهذا يندفع ما قِبْلَ: إنَّ هذا واضح لو كانَ التَّعريف مقصوداً بالذَّاتِ، وهو مُخالِفٌ لِمَا أسلفَهُ مِن أنَّ المقصود هُنَا التَّقسيم؛ حيث قال: ولَمَّا كانَ بيان الحاجة . . . إلخ)، وقولُ ذلكَ القائل: إِنَّ التَّقاسيمَ إِنَّما يُنظرُ فيها للذَّاتِ دونَ المفاهيم؛ ممنوع، بل النَّظرُ فيها للمفاهيم أيضاً، قال أبو الفتح: المقسم لا يكون إلا المفهوم ا.هـ. وفي الحواشي العماديَّة؛ أنَّ معنى قولهم: إِنَّ التَّقسيم بحسبِ الذَّاتِ؛ هو أَنَّ الباعث عليه هو حصولُ الذَّاتِ الَّتي هي لِلأقسام؛ لا أنَّ المقسَّم هو الذات ا.هـ. لا يقال قولُ الشَّارحِ فيما بعد وقدَّمَ في الأقسام والأحكام؛ لأنها بحسب الذَّاتِ صريح فيما قاله ذلك القائلُ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ: معنى ذلكَ أنَّهُ قدَّمَ الكلام على قسم التَّصورِ في المتن على قسم التصديقِ؛ حيثُ شرحَ أحوالَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمسِ، وقسَّمَها للجنس والفصل ... إلخ، وكذلكَ التَّعريفات وقسمها للحدّ والرَّسم إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ التَّقاسيم؛ كتقسيم الكُلِّيّ إلى ما له أفراد وما لا أفراد له، وإلى ذاتي وعَرَضي، وكذلكَ أحكامُها؛ أي: بيانُ مَا يعرضُ للتَّصوراتِ مِنَ الأحوالِ؛ نظراً إلى أنَّ ذاتَ التَّصورِ مُتقدِّم على ذاتِ التصديق؛ لأنَّ التصديق مُتوفِّفٌ عليه توقُفاً طبيعيًا كما علمت، وليس المعنى أنَّهُ فيما سيأتي يقعُ التَّصوُّرُ قسيماً للتّصديق، ويكونُ التَّصور سابقاً؛ لأنَّه لم يقع له فيما بعد ذلكَ التَّقسيم أصلاً؛ بل إنَّما وقعَ مِنْهُ كَغَيرِهِ مِنَ المُؤلِّفِينَ فِي مفتتح المقدمة فقط، وهو هذا الموضِعُ
Introduction of the author: I say: If by presenting conception before assent you mean that its essence is prior to assent, then it is granted, but it is not useful, because presenting assent here is in the definition. Dassuqi: Know that what is meant by 'position' in their statement 'conception is presented before assent in position, mention, writing, learning, and teaching.' (His saying: 'If you mean' i.e., you intended by your saying 'conception is presented before assent'). (His saying: 'that its essence' i.e., that its individuals are prior to the individuals of assent, and his saying: 'then it is granted' i.e., it is granted that conception according to its essence is prior to assent according to its essence. So by essence is meant individuals, and it is valid that by its essence is meant itself, i.e., that its existence is prior to the existence of assent, i.e., if you mean that its existence is prior to the existence of assent, then it is granted. (His saying: 'but it') i.e., the mentioned presentation, which is the presentation of conception before assent according to essence. (His saying: 'not useful') i.e., it does not benefit the questioner, i.e., the objector who says that it is better for the author to present conception before assent. (His saying: 'because presenting assent here in the definition' i.e., the definition of conception and assent implicit in the division. Attar: Upon denial, he is heedless of the terminology of the [؟]; for divisions like definitions do not prevent, and are only spoken about by way of invalidation as established in its place. (His saying: 'but it is not useful') i.e., the mentioned syllogism is granted, but its result does not contravene the purpose, because it only produced that the essence of conception, i.e., its individuals and referents, are prior to assent, and the discussion is not about that. And his saying after: 'And if by it you mean its concept... etc.' i.e., then the syllogism is incomplete by preventing the minor premise, i.e., we do not grant that the concept of conception is prior to the concept of assent, and the discussion here is about its concept, not its individuals. (His saying: 'because presenting assent here') i.e., in the definition that is included in the division, and the commentator's saying: 'And when the explanation of need... etc.' does not negate that the definition is also intended. [PAGE_130] The definition is not according to essence, but according to concept. And if by it you mean that its concept is prior to the concept of assent, then it is denied. Dassuqi: (His saying: 'And the definition is not according to essence, but according to concept.') By concept is meant the universal meaning which is affirmation of the predicative relation for assent, and non-affirmation of it for conception. And since the definition is according to concept, what is appropriate is what the author did of presenting assent before conception. Attar: Like the division, because he only explained the aspect of the purpose of the division, that the explanation of need depends on it, and by this is repelled what was said before: that this is clear if the definition was intended essentially, and it contradicts what he previously stated that the purpose here is division, where he said: 'And when the explanation of need... etc.' And the statement of that speaker: 'Divisions are only considered by essence, not concepts' is denied; rather, consideration of them is also for concepts. Abu al-Fath said: 'The divided is nothing but the concept.' End quote. And in the 'Amadiyya glosses: that the meaning of their statement 'division according to essence' is that the motivation for it is the attainment of the essence that is for the divisions, not that the divided is the essence. End quote. It cannot be said that the commentator's statement later 'And he presented in divisions and rulings, because they are according to essence' is explicit in what that speaker said, for we say: the meaning of that is that he presented the speech on the division of conception in the text before the division of assent, when he explained the states of the five universals and divided them into genus and species... etc., and also definitions and divided them into definition (hadd) and description (rasm), and other divisions like division of the universal into what has individuals and what has no individuals, into essential and accidental, and also their rulings, i.e., the explanation of what occurs to the conceptions of states, considering that the essence of conception is prior to the essence of assent, because assent depends on it by natural dependence as you learned. And the meaning is not that later on conception occurs as a division of assent, and conception is prior, because it did not occur for him after that division at all; rather, it only occurred from him, like other authors, at the opening of the introduction, and this is the place.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection. The objector claims that the author should have presented tasawwur (conception) before tasdiq (assent) in the definition. The commentator clarifies two possible meanings of 'prior': priority by essence/existence and priority by concept. If the objector means priority by essence (i.e., the individuals or existence of tasawwur are prior), this is accepted but irrelevant because the definition here is not about individuals but about concepts. If the objector means priority by concept (i.e., the universal meaning of tasawwur is prior to that of tasdiq), this is denied because the definition in this context is about the concepts themselves. The commentator then explains that the author's order (tasdiq before tasawwur) is appropriate because the definition is by concept, not by essence. Further, the discussion of divisions also concerns concepts, as clarified by citing Abu al-Fath and the 'Amadiyya glosses. The commentator concludes with a detailed explanation of the author's later presentation, showing that the priority of tasawwur in essence does not conflict with the order in the text. This lesson guards against conflating existential priority with conceptual priority in definitions.

التصوُّر
التصديق
الذات
المفهوم
التقسيم

Checkpoint

A) Because tasdiq is existentially prior to tasawwur
B) Because the definition here is by concept, not by essence
C) Because tasawwur and tasdiq are identical in meaning
D) Because the author made a mistake and the commentator is correcting it

Key Takeaway
In definitions and logical divisions, priority is determined by conceptual dependency, not existential order.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
10 min
The Three Restrictions and Ordering of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Commentary explaining three restrictions and ordering of tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three restrictions in the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) as assent to a predicative relation and explain the order of tasawwur and tasdiq in definition versus divisions.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is existence-based; tasawwur is absence-based; order depends on concept or essence.

In this passage, the commentators analyze the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) as idh'an li-nisba hukmiyya (assent to a predicative relation). They extract three restrictions (quyud) that make this definition precise. Additionally, they explain why tasdiq is defined before tasawwur, yet tasawwur is mentioned first in the divisions of knowledge. Understanding this ordering reveals a key epistemological distinction between conception and assent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three restrictions in the definition of 'ilm
Locate the three restrictions mentioned by Dusuqi: idh'an (assent), nisba (relation), and hukmiyya (predicative). They are the essential components of the definition of 'ilm.
The 'al' in al-quyud is for genus, not plurality.
2
Understand why the restrictions are called existence-based for tasdiq and non-existence-based for tasawwur
In tasdiq, the restrictions are positively asserted (e.g., assent is present), while in tasawwur, they are absent (no assent). Hence tasdiq is existence-based (wujudiyya) and tasawwur is non-existence-based ('adamiyya).
The concept of existence is prior to non-existence.
3
Explain the two orders: in definition vs. in divisions
In definition, tasdiq comes first because its restrictions are existence-based, and existence is conceptually prior. In divisions (like when we discuss the subject, predicate, and judgment), tasawwur comes first because we must conceive something before we can judge it.
The order of definition follows the concept (mafhum); the order of divisions follows the essence (dhat/individuals).
4
Recognize how the commentators extract the three restrictions from the matn
The matn says 'ilm is idh'an li-nisba hukmiyya. Attar explains that this phrase implicitly contains three restrictions: the term 'idh'an', 'nisba', and 'hukmiyya'. The plural 'quyud' is used generically to refer to these three.
This shows careful exegesis of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن
لأنَّ القيود في مفهوم التصديقِ وُجوديَّةٌ، وفي مفهوم التَّصورِ عَدَمِيَّةٌ، وتصور الوجودِ سابق على تصور العدم.
• فأَخَرَ التَّصورَ في التَّعريف؛ لأنَّه، بحسب المفهوم .
. وقدَّمَ في الأقسام والأحكام؛ لأنَّها سَبِ الذَّاتِ
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : الْقُيُوْدَ) أل للجنس، فتبطل معنى الجمعية، فتصدقُ بالواحد المراد هنا، وهو إذعانُ النِّسبةِ الحكميَّةِ، على أنَّ هذا لا يحتاج إليه ؛ لأنَّ عندنا قيوداً ؛ الأول : قوله : : إذعان، الثَّاني : قوله : النِّسبة، الثالث: قوله : الحكمية .
(قَوْلُهُ : عَدَمِيَّةٌ) أي : منسوبة للعدم، والمراد العدم المضافُ لَا المطلق، والقيود العدمية هنا ؛ عدم إذعانِ النّسبة الحكميَّةِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَتَصَوُّرُ الْوُجُوْدِ) أي : وجود شيء.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى تَصَوُّرِ الْعَدَمِ) أي: على تصوُّرِ عدمِ ذلكَ الشَّيء .
(قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدَّمَ) أي : التَّصَوُّر في الأقسام؛ أي: في طلب ذكرها حيث قالُوا : تصوُّر الموضوع، وتصور المحمول، وتصور النسبة... إلخ، فالمراد أقسام الإدراك .
(قَوْلُهُ: وَالْأَحْكَام أي: إنَّا إذا حكمنا على شيءٍ بشيءٍ؛ فإنَّا نتصوَّرُهُ أَوَّلاً ، ثمَّ نحكم عليه هذا حاصلُ مَا ارتضاه شيخُنَا سيدي مُحمَّد الصَّغير . اهـ شيخنا
(قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهَا أي: الأقسام والأحكام، وقوله: (بحسبِ الذَّات)؛ أي: الأفراد لا بحسب المفهوم. ؛
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّ الْقُيُوْدَ ...إلخ) هي ثلاثةٌ مأخوذةٌ مِن مجموع كلامِ المصنّفِ والشَّارحِ، فإنَّ قولَه : العِلْمُ إنْ كانَ إذعاناً للنسبةِ الحُكميَّةِ؛ يتضمَّن أَنَّ العِلمَ مُقيَّدٌ بكونه إذعاناً ، وكُونُ ذلكَ الإذعانِ مُتعلّقاً بالنّسبةِ، وكَونُ تلك النسبة مُحكميَّة، وحينئذ فالجمع باقي على معناه، وسقط ما أطالوا به هنا، ومعنى كون تلك القيود وجوديَّة؛ أنَّهُ لمْ يسلّط عليها حرفَ النَّفي كما في جانبِ التَّصور .
Introduction of the Matn Author: Because the restrictions in the concept of tasdiq are existence-based, and in the concept of tasawwur are non-existence-based, and the conception of existence precedes the conception of non-existence. So he placed tasawwur after in the definition because it is according to concept. And he placed it before in the divisions and rulings because they are according to the essence. Al-Dusuqi: (His saying: 'the restrictions') the 'al' is for the genus, so it invalidates the meaning of plurality, so it applies to the single one intended here, which is the assent to the predicative relation, although this is not needed because we have three restrictions: first, his saying 'assent', second, his saying 'relation', third, his saying 'predicative'. (His saying: 'non-existence-based') i.e., attributed to non-existence, and what is meant is relative non-existence, not absolute, and the non-existent restrictions here are the absence of assent to the predicative relation. (His saying: 'the conception of existence') i.e., the existence of a thing. (His saying: 'on the conception of non-existence') i.e., on the conception of the absence of that thing. (His saying: 'and he placed before') i.e., tasawwur in the divisions, i.e., in requesting their mention where they said: conception of the subject, conception of the predicate, conception of the relation... etc. So what is meant is the divisions of perception. (His saying: 'and the rulings') i.e., when we judge a thing by a thing, we first conceive it, then judge it. This is the conclusion approved by our master Muhammad al-Saghir. End of our master. (His saying: 'because they') i.e., the divisions and rulings, and his saying: 'according to the essence' i.e., according to the individuals, not according to the concept. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'because the restrictions... etc.') They are three taken from the combined words of the author and the commentator. For his saying: 'knowledge, if it is assent to the predicative relation' implies that knowledge is restricted by being assent, and that this assent is related to the relation, and that relation is predicative. Thus the plural remains on its meaning, and what they elaborated on here falls away. And the meaning of those restrictions being existence-based is that the negative particle was not applied to them as in the side of tasawwur.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological commentary on the matn's introduction about the definitions of tasawwur and tasdiq. The matn author explains why tasawwur is placed after tasdiq in the definition but before it in the divisions. The reason is that in definition, the order follows the concept: tasdiq's restrictions (the three qayd) are existence-based (wujudiyya), while tasawwur's are non-existence-based ('adamiyya). Since existence is conceptually prior to non-existence, tasdiq is defined first. However, in the divisions and rulings (e.g., when we speak of the object of judgment), tasawwur is prior because we must conceive something before we can assent to a judgment about it; this order follows the essence (dhat) of the mental acts, not the concept. The commentators then clarify the three restrictions (quyud) that constitute the definition of 'ilm as idh'an li-nisba hukmiyya: (1) idh'an (assent), (2) nisba (relation), (3) hukmiyya (predicative). Dusuqi explains that the word 'quyud' with the definite article for genus does not require plurality because it applies to a single instance; nevertheless, the definition indeed contains three distinct restrictions. Attar further explains that these three restrictions are derived from the combined expressions of the author and commentator. He also clarifies what 'wujudiyya' means: the restrictions are not negated; they are positively asserted, unlike the case of tasawwur which is defined simply as the absence of assent (a negative concept). This is why tasdiq is placed first in definition.

Key Terms
قيد (qayd)
إذعان (idh'an)
نسبة حكمية (nisba hukmiyya)
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)

According to the commentators, how many restrictions (quyud) are in the definition of 'ilm as idh'an li-nisba hukmiyya?
A) One: idh'an
B) Two: idh'an and nisba
C) Three: idh'an, nisba, and hukmiyya
D) Four: idh'an, nisba, hukmiyya, and existence

Key Takeaway
The three restrictions (idh'an, nisba, hukmiyya) make the definition of 'ilm precise, and the order of tasawwur and tasdiq differs depending on whether we follow the concept or the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate

Restricting the Necessary-Acquired Division to Temporal Knowledge
Refinement of necessary/acquired division scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of knowledge fall under the necessary/acquired division and explain why eternal and presential knowledge are excluded.

Core Idea
The division into necessary and acquired knowledge only applies to temporal, created knowledge, not to God's eternal knowledge.

The division of conception and assent into necessary and acquired is a foundational tool in Islamic logic. However, this division is not universal; it applies only to temporal cognitive acts. The super-commentator refines the scope, clarifying that eternal knowledge (like God's knowledge) and presential knowledge (like self-evident truths) cannot be described as acquired, because acquisition requires temporal intellectual motion. This lesson walks through the reasoning that restricts the division, preventing a category mistake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division under refinement
The passage is about the division of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī) as applied to conception and assent. Note that this division is described as 'accompanied by necessity' (bi-al-ḍarūra), meaning it is self-evident or based on immediate knowledge.
The author prefers interpreting 'ḍarūra' as self-evidence (badāha) rather than doubt (ṭaʿn).
2
Understand the relation between necessary and acquired
The relation between necessary and acquired is not contradictory (affirmation-negation), because not everything is subject to that pair (e.g., unseen existences). Instead, it is either contrariety (taḍādd) or privation/possession (ʿadam wa malaka). In both cases, it is possible for something described by one to potentially be described by the other.
This logical clarification helps avoid the false assumption that all knowledge must be either necessary or acquired.
3
Recognize the restriction to temporal knowledge
The passage argues that immediate/presential knowledge (e.g., knowledge by presence) and eternal knowledge (e.g., God's knowledge) cannot be described as acquired, because acquisition implies that knowledge is obtained after intellectual motion. Since eternal knowledge has no beginning, it cannot be acquired.
Thus, the division must be restricted to temporal (ḥādith) conception and assent – i.e., created human knowledge.
4
Apply the restriction to an example
Consider God's knowledge of the world: it is eternal, so it cannot be called 'acquired' (it does not come after thought). Therefore, it does not fall under the necessary/acquired division. Similarly, a newborn's innate knowledge of the impossibility of contradictions is presential and non-acquired, but it is temporal; it could be considered necessary. The division thus applies only to temporal knowledge, but even among temporal knowledge, some is necessary and some acquired.
This prevents confusion in theological discussions about divine attributes.

أي : التَصوُّرُ والتَّصديقُ (بِالضَّرُورَةِ)؛ أي: انقساماً ملتبساً بالضرورة، ثمَّ يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالضَّرورة البداهة، وأن يكون المراد بها التَّطْعَ، والظَّاهِرُ الأول، وحينئذ؛ فقولُ الشَّارح : وإِنَّما كان تقسيم ... إلخ، من باب التنبيه لا من باب البرهان ... قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : التَّصَوُّرُ والتَّصْدِيقُ) تقديمُ التَّصوُّرِ هنا على التصديق دون العكس المصنِّفُ؛ لأنَّ المنظور إليهِ هَهُنا ماصدقهما وأفرادهما، فإنَّهُ الَّذي يوصفُ بالضَّرورةِ والكَسْبِ ، وأمَّا مفهومُهُما ؛ فهو نظري كما سيأتي، قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّم العلوم: ليس بين الضَّروري والكسبي تقابلُ الإيجابِ والسَّلبِ؛ لأنَّ المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسَّلبِ لا يخلو موضوع ما عنهُما ، وإلا ؛ لزم ارتفاعُ النَّقيضين، والموجوداتُ الغيبيَّةُ كُلُّها خالية عنهما، ولا التّضايفُ، وهو ظاهر، بل إنَّما يتصوَّرُ التّضادُّ أو العدمُ والمَلَكةُ، ولا بُدَّ في التَّضادّ من إمكان تعاقبهما على موضوع واحدٍ، فكلُّ مَا يَتَّصِفُ بأحدِ الضَّدَّينِ ؛ أمكن انصافه بالآخَرِ، ولا بُدَّ في المتقابلين بالعدمِ والملكة من إمكانِ اتِّصافِ موضوعِ العدمِ بالمَلَكَةِ، وعلى التَّقديريْنِ ؛ فلا بُدَّ مِن إمكانِ اتِّصافِ الضَّروريّ بالكسبيَّةِ ، ومِنَ البَيْنِ أنَّ الحضوري يمتنع انصافُهُ بالكسبيَّة، وكذا القديمُ مِنَ العِلْم ؛ إذْ لو أمكن كونُهُ كَسبيًّا؛ لأمكن حصوله بعد الحركة الفكرية، فلا يكون قديماً هف، فَإِذَنْ؛ لا بُدَّ مِن تَخصيص المقسَّمِ بالتَّصورِ والتصديق الحادثين.
Meaning: [the division of] conception and assent (by necessity); i.e., a division accompanied by necessity. Then it is possible that what is meant by necessity is self-evidence, or that it means doubt/criticism? The apparent is the first. At that point, the commentator's statement 'And the division is only...' etc. is from the category of notification, not proof. ... His statement: 'i.e., conception and assent' — the author preceded conception here over assent, not the opposite, because what is looked at here is their referents and individuals, for they are described by necessity and acquisition. As for their concepts, they are theoretical as will come. The commentator of Sullam al-'Ulum said: There is not between the necessary and the acquired a relation of affirmation-negation; because from the two opposites by affirmation-negation nothing is free, otherwise the two contradictories would be absent. And the unseen existences are all free from them. Nor is it a relation of correlation, that is apparent. Rather, only contrariety or privation/possession is conceivable. In contrariety, it is necessary that they alternate over one subject; so everything that is described by one of the two contraries can be described by the other. And in the case of the two opposites by privation and possession, it is necessary that the subject of privation can be described by the possession. On both hypotheses, it is necessary that the necessary can be described by acquisition. But it is clear that immediate/present knowledge cannot be described by acquisition, and likewise eternal knowledge; for if it could be acquired, it would be possible to obtain it after intellectual motion, so it would not be eternal. Therefore, the division must be restricted to temporal conception and assent.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the super-commentator (hashiya) addressing the division of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī) as it applies to conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq). The author begins by clarifying that the phrase 'by necessity' indicates the division is accompanied by necessity, and that the preferred interpretation of 'darurah' here is self-evidence (badāha) rather than doubt (taʿn). He notes that the commentator's subsequent discussion is not a proof but a notification. The passage then explains why conception is mentioned before assent: because the division describes their referents (individual instances), not their concepts, which are theoretical. A key objection is addressed: the relation between necessary and acquired is not that of affirmation-negation (contradiction), because not all things fall under that pair; for instance, unseen existences are free from both. Instead, the relation is either contrariety (taḍādd) or privation/possession (ʿadam wa malaka). In both cases, it is possible for something described by one opposite to be described by the other. However, immediate (presential) knowledge and eternal knowledge cannot be described as acquired, since acquisition requires temporal intellectual motion. Therefore, the division must be restricted to temporal (ḥādith) conception and assent. This clarification prevents the misunderstanding that all knowledge, including God's knowledge, can be divided into necessary and acquired.

Key Terms
الضَّرورة
التَّصوُّر
التَّصديق
الكَسْب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the division of knowledge into necessary and acquired be restricted to temporal (ḥādith) conception and assent?
A) Because eternal knowledge cannot be described as acquired, as acquisition requires temporal intellectual motion.
B) Because all knowledge is either necessary or acquired without exception.
C) Because the terms necessary and acquired are purely linguistic and have no real meaning.
D) Because temporal knowledge is always acquired, never necessary.

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into necessary and acquired is restricted to temporal cognitive acts; eternal and presential knowledge are beyond this classification.

Why Self-Evident Premises Are Necessary to Avoid Infinite Regress
Authors defend definition of necessary knowledge using conditional argument against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument that if the premises of a proof are not claimed to be self-evident, then the proof leads to an infinite regress, invalidating it.

Core Idea
Without self-evident premises, every proof collapses into infinite regress or circularity.

In this passage, the commentator defends the definition of necessary knowledge against the accusation of circularity. The key move is to insist that the premises of the proof are self-evident. Why is this necessary? Because if the premises were theoretical, the opponent could demand a proof for each premise, leading to an infinite chain. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum: if not self-evident, then impossible; therefore they must be self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Al-‘Aṭṭār raises that defining necessary knowledge seems impossible because it uses premises that themselves require proof. What exactly is the problem?
2
Find the conditional argument
The reply states: if premises are not claimed to be self-evident, then the opponent can argue they are theoretical, leading to infinite regress or circularity (the opponent's own position). This makes inference impossible. Identify the 'if...then...' structure.
The Arabic: 'وإلا لقال الخصم...' meaning 'otherwise the opponent would say...'
3
Understand the impossibility
Infinite regress and circularity are both impossible in a valid argument. Therefore, the only way to avoid this is to claim self-evidence for the premises. The claim of badāha blocks the opponent's move.
4
Recognize the dialectical context
The commentator notes that this entire discussion is jadalī (dialectical) because the desired truth is self-evident and needs no proof. The argument is a defensive strategy, not a demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المكتسب، وإليه يُشيرُ الشَّارحُ بقوله : وهي التي لا يتوقَّفُ... إلخ، وبقوله : وهو ما يُخالفُ الضَّرورة ... إلخ؛ إذ المتوقفُ إنَّما هو المكتسب لا الاكتساب، والذي لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء؛ إنَّما هو الضَّروري لا الضَّرورة، وبقوله: وإِنَّما كان تقسيم التَّصورِ والتّصديقِ إلى الضّروريّ والنَّظريّ. العطار بهذه المقدّماتِ مُحَالاً، وأجابوا بأنَّ هذه المقدّماتِ وتصوراتها أُمورٌ معلومةٌ لنا بِلَا شُبهةٍ في ذلك فيتم الاستدلال، وهذا معنى قوله : لا يتم إلا بدعوى البداهة في الدَّليل وأطرافِهِ، وذلكَ كافٍ، لكنَّ الجَلالَ عَدَلَ عَن قولهم: معلوميَّة المقدّماتِ لبداهتها ؛ فَلِذَلِكَ اعترضَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الدَّليل لا يتم إلا بدعوى البداهة في مقدّماتِه وأطرافِهَا ؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما يتوقَّفُ على معلوميَّة المقدّماتِ وأطرافها، وأما على بداهة المقدّماتِ وأطرافها ؛ فَلَا ؛ فَضلا عن دعوى بداهتها، وأجاب عنه بعضُ الفُضَلاء بأنَّهُ لا يتم الاستدلال إلا بدعوى البداهة، وإلا ؛ لَقَالَ الخصم : إِنَّ هذهِ المقدِّماتِ مع أطرافها نظريَّةٌ على تقديرِ نظريَّةِ كُلِّ التَّصوراتِ والتصديقاتِ، فيحتاج في تحصيل هذه المقدّماتِ وأطرافها إلى الدَّورِ أو التسلسل المحالين، فيكونُ الاستدلال الموقوفُ عليهما محالاً، فإذا ادَّعَى بداهتها، لا يبقى للخصم مجال ادّعاءِ هذا المحالِ، ويتم الاستدلال، وأمَّا كَونُ بداهة تلك المقدِّماتِ معَ أَطرافها منافيةً لغرض نظريَّةِ كُلِّ التَّصوُّراتِ والتصديقاتِ ؛ فَلَا يضرُّ المستدلَّ ؛ بل يؤيّدُهُ، فَمَا ذَكَرَهُ مير أبو الفتح مبني على عدمِ التَّفرقةِ بينَ الدَّليل والاستدلال ا.هـ. ، والفرقُ بينَهُما أَنَّ الدَّليل أعمُ مِنَ الاستدلال؛ لأنَّ الاستدلال ما يكونُ مُقدِّماتُهُ بديهيَّةٌ، والدَّليلُ ما يكونُ مُقدِّماتُهُ معلومة بديهية أو لا، ومِن لَطَائِفِ مير زاهد ؛ مَا قالَ : إِنَّ هذا الحُكْمَ - يعني قول المصنف : ويقتسمان ... إلخ - نظير المثبتِ لِنفْسِهِ، فَإِنَّه إِنْ كان بديهيا ؛ كانَ نَفياً لنظريَّةِ الكُلِّ ، وإنْ كانَ نَظريًّا ؛ كانَ نَفياً لبداهةِ الكُلِّ ا.هـ وَنِعْمَ مَا قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّم العُلوم بعدَ أنْ ساقَ نحوَ مَا ذَكَرْنَا : والحَقُّ أَنَّ هذا كُلَّهُ جَدَلي، والمطلوب ضروري لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال .ا.هـ.
The acquired knowledge, and to it the commentator refers by his saying: 'It is that which does not depend... etc.' and by his saying: 'It is that which contradicts the necessary... etc.'; for the dependent is only the acquired, not the acquisition, and that which does not depend on anything is only the necessary, not necessity. And by his saying: 'The division of conception and assent into necessary and theoretical was only...' Al-‘Aṭṭār: With these premises [the definition] is impossible, and they answered that these premises and their concepts are known to us without any doubt in that, so the inference is completed. This is the meaning of his saying: 'It is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in the proof and its terms,' and that is sufficient. But al-Jalāl deviated from their saying: 'the knownness of the premises due to their self-evidence'; therefore Abū al-Fatḥ objected to him that we do not concede that the proof is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in its premises and their terms, because it only depends on the knownness of the premises and their terms, not on the self-evidence of the premises and their terms, let alone claiming their self-evidence. And some virtuosos answered him that inference is not completed except by claiming self-evidence; otherwise, the opponent would say: these premises together with their terms are theoretical on the assumption that all concepts and assents are theoretical, so in acquiring these premises and their terms, there would be need for circularity or infinite regress, both impossible, so the inference dependent on them would be impossible. Thus, if one claims their self-evidence, the opponent has no room to claim this impossibility, and the inference is completed. As for the fact that the self-evidence of those premises with their terms contradicts the purpose of all concepts and assents being theoretical, it does not harm the one inferring; rather it supports him. So what Mīr Abū al-Fatḥ mentioned is based on not distinguishing between the proof and inference. End. And the difference between them is that the proof is more general than inference, because inference is that whose premises are self-evident, while proof is that whose premises are known, whether self-evident or not. And among the subtleties of Mīr Zāhid is what he said: 'This ruling — meaning the author's statement: 'and they are divided...' — is similar to the self-affirmer; if it is self-evident, it is a negation of the theoretically of all, and if it is theoretical, it is a negation of the self-evidence of all.' End. And excellent is what the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulūm said after citing similar to what we mentioned: 'The truth is that all of this is dialectical, and the desired is necessary, not needing inference.' End.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological commentary on the definition of necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) and the need for self-evident premises in proof. The commentator begins by clarifying that the dependent (al-mutawaqqif) is the acquired knowledge, not the acquisition, and that which does not depend is the necessary, not necessity itself. Al-‘Aṭṭār then raises an objection: the definition of necessary knowledge seems impossible because it uses premises that themselves require proof. The response is that those premises are known to us without doubt—they are self-evident. Abū al-Fatḥ objects that the proof only requires knownness, not self-evidence. The reply is crucial: if the premises are not claimed to be self-evident, the opponent could argue that they are theoretical, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, which would make inference impossible. Therefore, claiming self-evidence is necessary to block this regress. The passage then distinguishes between proof (dalīl) and inference (istidlāl): inference has self-evident premises, while proof may have known premises (not necessarily self-evident). Mīr Zāhid notes that the ruling on the division of knowledge is self-referential—if self-evident, it negates the claim that all is theoretical; if theoretical, it negates the claim that all is self-evident. Finally, the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulūm declares the entire discussion dialectical, as the desired truth is self-evident and needs no proof. Throughout, the argument follows a conditional structure: if premises are not self-evident, then infinite regress follows, so they must be self-evident.

Key Terms
الضَّروري
النَّظري
البداهة
الدَّليل
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of not claiming self-evidence for the premises of a proof?
A) The premises become theoretical and the inference fails.
B) The opponent will argue that the premises lead to infinite regress or circularity, making inference impossible.
C) The proof becomes a matter of dialectic rather than demonstration.
D) The distinction between necessary and theoretical knowledge collapses.

Key Takeaway
A proof cannot avoid infinite regress unless its premises are claimed to be self-evident, blocking the opponent's demand for further justification.

Distinguishing Proof (Dalīl) from Inference (Istidlāl)
Authors distinguish between dalīl and istidlāl to resolve an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dalīl and istidlāl based on whether the premises are self-evident or merely known.

Core Idea
Inference requires self-evident premises; proof may rely on premises that are known but not self-evident.

In the passage, the distinction between dalīl and istidlāl is used to defend against an objection. The commentator explains that istidlāl (inference) is a proof whose premises are self-evident, while dalīl (proof) is more general, including premises that are known but not necessarily self-evident. This distinction helps clarify why claiming self-evidence is necessary specifically for istidlāl.

Guided Path
1
Locate the distinction in the text
Find where the commentator says 'والفرق بينهما أن الدليل أعم من الاستدلال'. This is the core of the difference.
2
Define each term
Istidlāl: proof whose premises are self-evident (badīha). Dalīl: proof whose premises are known, whether self-evident or not.
Note that 'known' may include both self-evident and theoretical premises.
3
Understand the scope
Dalīl is more general: it covers both self-evident premises and known premises. Istidlāl is a subset of dalīl where the premises are self-evident.
4
Apply to the earlier objection
Abū al-Fatḥ's objection is based on conflating dalīl and istidlāl. The commentator shows that the inference in question (istidlāl) requires self-evidence because it is a specific type of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المكتسب، وإليه يُشيرُ الشَّارحُ بقوله : وهي التي لا يتوقَّفُ... إلخ، وبقوله : وهو ما يُخالفُ الضَّرورة ... إلخ؛ إذ المتوقفُ إنَّما هو المكتسب لا الاكتساب، والذي لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء؛ إنَّما هو الضَّروري لا الضَّرورة، وبقوله: وإِنَّما كان تقسيم التَّصورِ والتّصديقِ إلى الضّروريّ والنَّظريّ. العطار بهذه المقدّماتِ مُحَالاً، وأجابوا بأنَّ هذه المقدّماتِ وتصوراتها أُمورٌ معلومةٌ لنا بِلَا شُبهةٍ في ذلك فيتم الاستدلال، وهذا معنى قوله : لا يتم إلا بدعوى البداهة في الدَّليل وأطرافِهِ، وذلكَ كافٍ، لكنَّ الجَلالَ عَدَلَ عَن قولهم: معلوميَّة المقدّماتِ لبداهتها ؛ فَلِذَلِكَ اعترضَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الدَّليل لا يتم إلا بدعوى البداهة في مقدّماتِه وأطرافِهَا ؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما يتوقَّفُ على معلوميَّة المقدّماتِ وأطرافها، وأما على بداهة المقدّماتِ وأطرافها ؛ فَلَا ؛ فَضلا عن دعوى بداهتها، وأجاب عنه بعضُ الفُضَلاء بأنَّهُ لا يتم الاستدلال إلا بدعوى البداهة، وإلا ؛ لَقَالَ الخصم : إِنَّ هذهِ المقدِّماتِ مع أطرافها نظريَّةٌ على تقديرِ نظريَّةِ كُلِّ التَّصوراتِ والتصديقاتِ، فيحتاج في تحصيل هذه المقدّماتِ وأطرافها إلى الدَّورِ أو التسلسل المحالين، فيكونُ الاستدلال الموقوفُ عليهما محالاً، فإذا ادَّعَى بداهتها، لا يبقى للخصم مجال ادّعاءِ هذا المحالِ، ويتم الاستدلال، وأمَّا كَونُ بداهة تلك المقدِّماتِ معَ أَطرافها منافيةً لغرض نظريَّةِ كُلِّ التَّصوُّراتِ والتصديقاتِ ؛ فَلَا يضرُّ المستدلَّ ؛ بل يؤيّدُهُ، فَمَا ذَكَرَهُ مير أبو الفتح مبني على عدمِ التَّفرقةِ بينَ الدَّليل والاستدلال ا.هـ. ، والفرقُ بينَهُما أَنَّ الدَّليل أعمُ مِنَ الاستدلال؛ لأنَّ الاستدلال ما يكونُ مُقدِّماتُهُ بديهيَّةٌ، والدَّليلُ ما يكونُ مُقدِّماتُهُ معلومة بديهية أو لا، ومِن لَطَائِفِ مير زاهد ؛ مَا قالَ : إِنَّ هذا الحُكْمَ - يعني قول المصنف : ويقتسمان ... إلخ - نظير المثبتِ لِنفْسِهِ، فَإِنَّه إِنْ كان بديهيا ؛ كانَ نَفياً لنظريَّةِ الكُلِّ ، وإنْ كانَ نَظريًّا ؛ كانَ نَفياً لبداهةِ الكُلِّ ا.هـ وَنِعْمَ مَا قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّم العُلوم بعدَ أنْ ساقَ نحوَ مَا ذَكَرْنَا : والحَقُّ أَنَّ هذا كُلَّهُ جَدَلي، والمطلوب ضروري لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال .ا.هـ.
The acquired knowledge, and to it the commentator refers by his saying: 'It is that which does not depend... etc.' and by his saying: 'It is that which contradicts the necessary... etc.'; for the dependent is only the acquired, not the acquisition, and that which does not depend on anything is only the necessary, not necessity. And by his saying: 'The division of conception and assent into necessary and theoretical was only...' Al-‘Aṭṭār: With these premises [the definition] is impossible, and they answered that these premises and their concepts are known to us without any doubt in that, so the inference is completed. This is the meaning of his saying: 'It is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in the proof and its terms,' and that is sufficient. But al-Jalāl deviated from their saying: 'the knownness of the premises due to their self-evidence'; therefore Abū al-Fatḥ objected to him that we do not concede that the proof is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in its premises and their terms, because it only depends on the knownness of the premises and their terms, not on the self-evidence of the premises and their terms, let alone claiming their self-evidence. And some virtuosos answered him that inference is not completed except by claiming self-evidence; otherwise, the opponent would say: these premises together with their terms are theoretical on the assumption that all concepts and assents are theoretical, so in acquiring these premises and their terms, there would be need for circularity or infinite regress, both impossible, so the inference dependent on them would be impossible. Thus, if one claims their self-evidence, the opponent has no room to claim this impossibility, and the inference is completed. As for the fact that the self-evidence of those premises with their terms contradicts the purpose of all concepts and assents being theoretical, it does not harm the one inferring; rather it supports him. So what Mīr Abū al-Fatḥ mentioned is based on not distinguishing between the proof and inference. End. And the difference between them is that the proof is more general than inference, because inference is that whose premises are self-evident, while proof is that whose premises are known, whether self-evident or not. And among the subtleties of Mīr Zāhid is what he said: 'This ruling — meaning the author's statement: 'and they are divided...' — is similar to the self-affirmer; if it is self-evident, it is a negation of the theoretically of all, and if it is theoretical, it is a negation of the self-evidence of all.' End. And excellent is what the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulūm said after citing similar to what we mentioned: 'The truth is that all of this is dialectical, and the desired is necessary, not needing inference.' End.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological commentary on the definition of necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) and the need for self-evident premises in proof. The commentator begins by clarifying that the dependent (al-mutawaqqif) is the acquired knowledge, not the acquisition, and that which does not depend is the necessary, not necessity itself. Al-‘Aṭṭār then raises an objection: the definition of necessary knowledge seems impossible because it uses premises that themselves require proof. The response is that those premises are known to us without doubt—they are self-evident. Abū al-Fatḥ objects that the proof only requires knownness, not self-evidence. The reply is crucial: if the premises are not claimed to be self-evident, the opponent could argue that they are theoretical, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, which would make inference impossible. Therefore, claiming self-evidence is necessary to block this regress. The passage then distinguishes between proof (dalīl) and inference (istidlāl): inference has self-evident premises, while proof may have known premises (not necessarily self-evident). Mīr Zāhid notes that the ruling on the division of knowledge is self-referential—if self-evident, it negates the claim that all is theoretical; if theoretical, it negates the claim that all is self-evident. Finally, the commentator of Sullam al-‘Ulūm declares the entire discussion dialectical, as the desired truth is self-evident and needs no proof. Throughout, the argument follows a conditional structure: if premises are not self-evident, then infinite regress follows, so they must be self-evident.

Key Terms
الدَّليل
الاستدلال
البداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Dalīl is a subset of istidlāl.
C) Istidlāl is a subset of dalīl.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Istidlāl is a proof with self-evident premises, while dalīl is any proof with known premises, making istidlāl a subset of dalīl.

The Epistemological Division: Necessary vs Acquired Knowledge
Author defines necessary and acquired knowledge with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given piece of knowledge as necessary or acquired based on its dependence on reflection (naẓar).

Core Idea
Knowledge is either self-evident or requires reflection; this shapes all Islamic proof.

In the Islamic intellectual tradition, knowledge is not a monolith. Some truths are immediately recognized, while others require a chain of reasoning. This lesson unpacks the foundational division of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī) types, and explains how reflection (naẓar) transforms a theoretical conception into a self-evident judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and locate the two kinds of knowledge: necessary and acquired. Note that acquired knowledge is obtained through reflection (naẓar).
2
Understand the mechanism
For a judgment to be necessary, the conception of its terms must be theoretical, but the judgment itself becomes self-evident once the terms are understood. Follow the example of the possible (al-mumkin) needing a determinant.
3
Distinguish between conception and assent
Recognize that conception (taṣawwur) can be necessary or theoretical, and assent (taṣdīq) can also be necessary or theoretical. The passage emphasizes that even theoretical conceptions can lead to necessary judgments.
4
Apply the classification
Given a statement, determine whether it is necessary or acquired by checking if it requires reflection. Use the examples from the passage: 'negation and affirmation cannot coexist' is necessary; 'the world is originated' is acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَى الِاكْتِسَابِ بِالنَّظَرِ وَهُوَ مَا يُخَالِفُ الضَّرُورَةَ. ضَرُورِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّ مَنْ تَصَوَّرَ الْمُمْكِنَ بِأَنَّهُ مَا تَسَاوَى وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِذَاتِهِ، وَالِاحْتِيَاجَ بِأَنَّهُ الِافْتِقَارُ إِلَى مَنْ يُرَجِّحُ أَحَدَهُمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ جَزَمَ بِثُبُوتِ الِاحْتِيَاجِ إِلَى الْمُمْكِنِ. فَكُلٌّ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ نَظَرِيٌّ، وَالْحُكْمُ بَدِيهِيٌّ. وَهَذَا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْحُكَمَاءِ مِنْ أَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ هُوَ الْحُكْمُ، وَأَنَّهُ بَسِيطٌ. وَأَمَّا عَلَى أَنَّهُ مُرَكَّبٌ؛ فَهُوَ نَظَرِيٌّ كَمَا مَرَّ. (قَوْلُهُ: كَتَصَوُّرِ الْحَرَارَةِ) أَيْ: بِوَجْهٍ مَا كَتَصَوُّرِهَا بِأَنَّهَا كَيْفِيَّةٌ تُسَخِّنُ الْجِسْمَ، وَتَصَوُّرِ الْبُرُودَةِ بِأَنَّهَا كَيْفِيَّةٌ تُبَرِّدُ الْجِسْمَ لَا بِالْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْكُنْهِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ نَظَرِيٌّ. (قَوْلُهُ: بِأَنَّ النَّفْيَ) أَيْ: بِأَنَّ انْتِفَاءَ شَيْءٍ عَنْ آخَرَ كَانْتِفَاءِ الْعَدَمِ عَنْ زَيْدٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالْإِثْبَاتَ؛ أَيْ: ثُبُوتَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ لِلْآخَرِ؛ أَيْ: ثُبُوتَ الْعَدَمِ لِزَيْدٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: (لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ وَلَا يَرْتَفِعَانِ) أَيْ: لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ مُوَافِقًا لِلْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْحَاصِلُ أَحَدُهُمَا وَهُوَ الْوَاقِعُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ كَالْقِيَامِ أَوْ عَدَمِهِ. وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ بِالنَّفْيِ إِدْرَاكَ أَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ لَيْسَتْ وَاقِعَةً عَلَى وَجْهِ الْجَزْمِ أَوِ الظَّنِّ، وَبِالْإِثْبَاتِ إِدْرَاكَ أَنَّهَا وَاقِعَةً عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الْمَذْكُورِ؛ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَهُمَا تَضَادًّا بِاعْتِبَارِ اتِّصَافِ النَّفْسِ بِهِمَا، فَيَرْتَفِعَانِ فِي صُورَةِ الشَّكِّ. وَمَثَّلَ الشَّارِحُ بِمِثَالَيْنِ؛ الْأَوَّلُ: لِلتَّصَوُّرِ الضَّرُورِيِّ، وَالثَّانِي: لِلتَّصْدِيقِ الضَّرُورِيِّ. 
وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ: كَتَصَوُّرِ الْعَقْلِ وَالْإِنْسَانِ. وَكَالتَّصْدِيقِ بِأَنَّ الْعَالَمَ حَادِثٌ.
And (knowledge is divided) into acquisition by reflection, which is what opposes necessity. It is necessary because whoever conceives the possible as that whose existence and non-existence are equal with respect to its essence, and need as the dependence upon one who gives preponderance to one of them over the other, then he judges with certainty that the possible has a need. So each of the two conceptions is theoretical, while the judgment is self-evident. This is according to the philosophers that assent is the judgment and that it is simple. But if it is considered composite, then it is theoretical as previously. (His saying: 'like the conception of heat') i.e., in some respect, like conceiving it as a quality that heats the body, and conceiving cold as a quality that cools the body, not in reality and essence; for that is theoretical. (His saying: 'that negation and affirmation do not coexist and cannot be removed') i.e., they do not coexist in the actual state of affairs in agreement with reality, but only one of them occurs, which is the actual state of affairs, such as standing or its absence. What is meant by negation is not the apprehension that the relation does not occur with certainty or opinion, and by affirmation the apprehension that it occurs in the mentioned manner; because there is opposition between them in terms of the soul's description by them, so they both cease in the case of doubt. The commentator gave two examples: the first for necessary conception, and the second for necessary assent. And from it his saying: like the conception of intellect and man. And like the assent that the world is originated.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī) types, with subcategories of conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq). The author (al-Dassūqī) is explaining the division by giving examples. First, he clarifies a point: if someone conceives the possible (al-mumkin) and its need for a determinant, the conception itself is theoretical (naẓarī), while the judgment that it is dependent is self-evident (badīhī). This shows that a theoretical conception can lead to a necessary assent. The author then gives examples of necessary knowledge: conception of heat and cold in a superficial way (since their essence is theoretical), and the assent that negation and affirmation cannot coexist or both be absent—this is the logical principle of non-contradiction. He explains that this is not merely subjective but corresponds to reality. Finally, he gives examples of acquired knowledge: the conception of intellect (al-ʿaql) as a power of the soul, and man as a rational animal; and the assent that the world is originated (ḥādith). The latter depends on a proof: the world is changeable, and everything changeable is originated. The passage underscores that knowledge can be self-evident or require reflection, and both types are essential in Islamic epistemology.

Conception and Assent: The Self-Evident in Action
Examples of necessary and acquired knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessary conception from necessary assent and identify examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge is argumentative; some is immediately present to the mind.

The passage provides concrete examples of necessary knowledge: the conception of heat and cold in a general way, and the assent that negation and affirmation cannot both be true. This lesson examines these examples to understand what makes knowledge self-evident. We also see how the philosophers' view of assent as simple affects the classification.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example
Read the passage's explanation of necessary conception of heat and cold. Note that it is necessary only in a superficial sense; the true essence of heat is theoretical.
2
Examine the second example
Understand the necessary assent of non-contradiction: the judgment that negation and affirmation cannot coexist or both be absent. This is a self-evident logical principle.
3
Compare with acquired knowledge
The passage mentions 'conception of intellect and man' and 'assent that the world is originated' as acquired. Reflect on why these require reflection: man as a rational animal requires composition; the world's origination requires a proof.
4
Note the philosophical nuance
The philosophers hold that assent is simple, making the judgment of non-contradiction self-evident. If assent were composite, even this would be theoretical. Understand the implication.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Commentator's Expansion: From Base Text to Clarification
Commentator expands on base text with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace how a commentator (shāriḥ) expands a base text's claim by adding examples and resolving ambiguities.

Core Idea
A commentator makes the implicit explicit, turning axioms into accessible examples.

This passage from al-Dassūqī's commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq illustrates how a commentator clarifies and elaborates on a condensed base text. The base text likely stated the division of knowledge briefly; al-Dassūqī provides concrete examples of necessary conception and assent, and even notes a point of philosophical disagreement. This lesson demonstrates the analytical value of the commentary tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
Assume the matn said something like 'Knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired.' Find the specific points in al-Dassūqī's commentary that likely respond to or expand that.
2
Find the examples added
Al-Dassūqī gives examples of necessary knowledge (heat, cold, non-contradiction) and acquired (man, world). These are expansions not in the base text.
3
Note the philosophical nuance
He mentions the philosophers' view that assent is simple, and the alternative that it is composite. This shows the commentator is addressing a potential objection or clarifying the implication.
4
Evaluate the commentary's effect
Consider how the examples and nuance deepen understanding. Without the commentary, a student might misunderstand what counts as necessary.

وَإِلَى الِاكْتِسَابِ بِالنَّظَرِ وَهُوَ مَا يُخَالِفُ الضَّرُورَةَ. ضَرُورِيٌّ؛ لِأَنَّ مَنْ تَصَوَّرَ الْمُمْكِنَ بِأَنَّهُ مَا تَسَاوَى وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِذَاتِهِ، وَالِاحْتِيَاجَ بِأَنَّهُ الِافْتِقَارُ إِلَى مَنْ يُرَجِّحُ أَحَدَهُمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ جَزَمَ بِثُبُوتِ الِاحْتِيَاجِ إِلَى الْمُمْكِنِ. فَكُلٌّ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ نَظَرِيٌّ، وَالْحُكْمُ بَدِيهِيٌّ. وَهَذَا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْحُكَمَاءِ مِنْ أَنَّ التَّصْدِيقَ هُوَ الْحُكْمُ، وَأَنَّهُ بَسِيطٌ. وَأَمَّا عَلَى أَنَّهُ مُرَكَّبٌ؛ فَهُوَ نَظَرِيٌّ كَمَا مَرَّ. (قَوْلُهُ: كَتَصَوُّرِ الْحَرَارَةِ) أَيْ: بِوَجْهٍ مَا كَتَصَوُّرِهَا بِأَنَّهَا كَيْفِيَّةٌ تُسَخِّنُ الْجِسْمَ، وَتَصَوُّرِ الْبُرُودَةِ بِأَنَّهَا كَيْفِيَّةٌ تُبَرِّدُ الْجِسْمَ لَا بِالْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْكُنْهِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ نَظَرِيٌّ. (قَوْلُهُ: بِأَنَّ النَّفْيَ) أَيْ: بِأَنَّ انْتِفَاءَ شَيْءٍ عَنْ آخَرَ كَانْتِفَاءِ الْعَدَمِ عَنْ زَيْدٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالْإِثْبَاتَ؛ أَيْ: ثُبُوتَ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ لِلْآخَرِ؛ أَيْ: ثُبُوتَ الْعَدَمِ لِزَيْدٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: (لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ وَلَا يَرْتَفِعَانِ) أَيْ: لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ مُوَافِقًا لِلْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْحَاصِلُ أَحَدُهُمَا وَهُوَ الْوَاقِعُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ كَالْقِيَامِ أَوْ عَدَمِهِ. وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ بِالنَّفْيِ إِدْرَاكَ أَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ لَيْسَتْ وَاقِعَةً عَلَى وَجْهِ الْجَزْمِ أَوِ الظَّنِّ، وَبِالْإِثْبَاتِ إِدْرَاكَ أَنَّهَا وَاقِعَةً عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الْمَذْكُورِ؛ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَهُمَا تَضَادًّا بِاعْتِبَارِ اتِّصَافِ النَّفْسِ بِهِمَا، فَيَرْتَفِعَانِ فِي صُورَةِ الشَّكِّ. وَمَثَّلَ الشَّارِحُ بِمِثَالَيْنِ؛ الْأَوَّلُ: لِلتَّصَوُّرِ الضَّرُورِيِّ، وَالثَّانِي: لِلتَّصْدِيقِ الضَّرُورِيِّ. 
وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ: كَتَصَوُّرِ الْعَقْلِ وَالْإِنْسَانِ. وَكَالتَّصْدِيقِ بِأَنَّ الْعَالَمَ حَادِثٌ.
And (knowledge is divided) into acquisition by reflection, which is what opposes necessity. It is necessary because whoever conceives the possible as that whose existence and non-existence are equal with respect to its essence, and need as the dependence upon one who gives preponderance to one of them over the other, then he judges with certainty that the possible has a need. So each of the two conceptions is theoretical, while the judgment is self-evident. This is according to the philosophers that assent is the judgment and that it is simple. But if it is considered composite, then it is theoretical as previously. (His saying: 'like the conception of heat') i.e., in some respect, like conceiving it as a quality that heats the body, and conceiving cold as a quality that cools the body, not in reality and essence; for that is theoretical. (His saying: 'that negation and affirmation do not coexist and cannot be removed') i.e., they do not coexist in the actual state of affairs in agreement with reality, but only one of them occurs, which is the actual state of affairs, such as standing or its absence. What is meant by negation is not the apprehension that the relation does not occur with certainty or opinion, and by affirmation the apprehension that it occurs in the mentioned manner; because there is opposition between them in terms of the soul's description by them, so they both cease in the case of doubt. The commentator gave two examples: the first for necessary conception, and the second for necessary assent. And from it his saying: like the conception of intellect and man. And like the assent that the world is originated.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (kasbī) types, with subcategories of conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq). The author (al-Dassūqī) is explaining the division by giving examples. First, he clarifies a point: if someone conceives the possible (al-mumkin) and its need for a determinant, the conception itself is theoretical (naẓarī), while the judgment that it is dependent is self-evident (badīhī). This shows that a theoretical conception can lead to a necessary assent. The author then gives examples of necessary knowledge: conception of heat and cold in a superficial way (since their essence is theoretical), and the assent that negation and affirmation cannot coexist or both be absent—this is the logical principle of non-contradiction. He explains that this is not merely subjective but corresponds to reality. Finally, he gives examples of acquired knowledge: the conception of intellect (al-ʿaql) as a power of the soul, and man as a rational animal; and the assent that the world is originated (ḥādith). The latter depends on a proof: the world is changeable, and everything changeable is originated. The passage underscores that knowledge can be self-evident or require reflection, and both types are essential in Islamic epistemology.

المتن
التوسعة
التمثيل
التنقيح

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
Distinguishing Tasawwur and Tasdiq: Conception vs Assent
Matn defines tasawwur and tasdiq with examples; al-Dasuqi explains each example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) in the Islamic logical tradition, and identify whether an example is a theoretical conception or theoretical assent.

Core Idea
Tasawwur grasps a concept; tasdiq affirms a judgment; both can be immediate or theoretical.

In Islamic logic, knowledge is divided into conception and assent. Conception is simply understanding a meaning, while assent is affirming a proposition. The matn provides examples, and al-Dasuqi explains that these are theoretical because they require proof or definition. Let's work through the passage to master this foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions in the matn
Read the matn's opening: it gives examples for tasawwur ('aql and insan) and tasdiq (the world is originated). Do not confuse examples with definitions; the definitions are understood from the tradition.
The matn itself does not state the definitions explicitly; it relies on prior knowledge.
2
Understand the nature of tasawwur and tasdiq
Tasawwur is the mental apprehension of a concept without any judgment. Tasdiq is the affirmation that a proposition is true or false. In the examples, 'conception of intellect' means understanding what 'intellect' is; 'assent that the world is originated' means judging that the world is created.
The examples are given in the form 'tasawwur X' and 'tasdiq that Y'.
3
Analyse why the examples are theoretical
Al-Dasuqi explains that these examples are not immediate (daruri) but theoretical (nazari). Conception of 'aql requires knowing it is a faculty of the soul; conception of 'insan' requires knowing it is a rational animal. Assent that the world is originated requires a proof: the world is changing, and everything changing is originated.
Theoretical knowledge depends on reflection and acquisition.
4
Classify additional examples
Apply the criteria: if an expression conveys a concept without judgment, it is tasawwur. If it involves a judgment that can be true or false, it is tasdiq. For instance, 'the world is originated' is a proposition; 'world' alone is a concept.
Practice by considering other Islamic theological statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن - كتصور : العقل والإنسان. - وكالتصديق : بأنَّ العالم حادث. الدسوقي الضَّرورة؛ أي: الضَّروريّ، فهو عِلْمٌ يتوقَّفُ حصوله على نَظَرٍ وكَشب؛ أي: الصُّورةُ الَّتي يتوقَّفُ حصولها في العقل على نَظَرِ وكَشب . (قَوْلُهُ : كَتَصَوُّرِ الْعَقْل أي : بأَنَّهُ قوَّةٌ لِلنَّفْسِ تَستعدُّ بها لإدراك المعلوم، وقوله : والإنسان)؛ أي: بأنَّهُ حيوان ناطق عدم، . (قَوْلُهُ : بِأَنَّ الْعَالَمَ) أي جواهر وأعراض وقوله : (حادث)؛ أي: موجودٌ بعد فإِنَّهُ مُتوقِّفٌ على إقامة دليل، وهو : العَالَمُ مُتغيّر، وكُلُّ مُتغيّر حادث، ومُثْلَ بثلاثة أمثلة ؛ الأَوَّليين : للتَّصورِ النَّظريّ، والثالث : للتصديقِ النَّظريّ.
Introduction of the author of the matn: - As an example of conception (tasawwur): al-‘aql (intellect) and al-insān (human). - And as an example of assent (tasdiq): that the world is originated (ḥādith). Al-Dasūqī: ‘al-ḍarūrah’ meaning the necessary; so it is knowledge the attainment of which depends on reflection and acquisition; meaning the form whose attainment in the intellect depends on reflection and acquisition. His statement: ‘like the conception of al-‘aql’ meaning that it is a faculty of the soul by which it is prepared to grasp the known, and his statement: ‘and al-insān’ meaning that it is a rational animal (ḥayawān nāṭiq). His statement: ‘that the world’ meaning substances and accidents, and his statement: ‘originated’ meaning existing after non-existence; for it depends on establishing a proof, namely: the world is changing, and everything changing is originated. And he gave three examples: the first two for theoretical conception, and the third for theoretical assent.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage from the introduction of the matn defines the two types of knowledge according to logicians: conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Tasawwur is simply grasping a concept, like understanding what 'intellect' or 'human' means. Tasdiq is affirming a judgment, such as 'the world is originated'. Al-Dasuqi then explains the matn's examples. He clarifies that the term 'al-ḍarūrah' (necessary) refers to knowledge that is immediate, whereas the examples given are of acquired knowledge, because they depend on a proof. To conceive 'intellect' one must know it is a faculty of the soul. To conceive 'human' one must know it is a rational animal. To assent to 'the world is originated' requires a proof: that the world is changing, and everything changing is originated. Thus the matn provides three examples: two of theoretical conception (intellect and human) and one of theoretical assent (the world's origination).

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
عقل (aql)
إنسان (insan)
عالم (alam)

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Proof by Contradiction for the Division of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Dassuqi explains the proof by contradiction for the division of concepts and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the conditional proof by contradiction (dalil al-khalf) for the division of concepts and assents into necessary and acquired.

Core Idea
If a claim leads to absurdity, its opposite is proven true.

This passage from Dassuqi's commentary demonstrates a foundational epistemological proof in Islamic logic: that both tasawwur (concepts) and tasdiq (assents) must divide into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). The proof uses a conditional syllogism with a contradiction to establish the division. Understanding this proof is crucial because it establishes that knowledge is not monolithic but has both intuitive and acquired components, which underlies the entire enterprise of reasoning ('ilm and nazar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional
The antecedent is 'if they were not divided', i.e., the negation of the thesis: 'tasawwur and tasdiq are not divided into necessary and acquired.' This is the position to be refuted.
The antecedent is explicitly given as 'لو لم ينقسما إليهما'.
2
Identify the consequent (tali) that follows from the antecedent
The consequent is 'then all individuals of tasawwur and tasdiq would be either entirely intuitive (badihi) or entirely acquired (kasbi).' This means each individual concept or assent would fall into one extreme category exclusively.
The consequent is an exclusive disjunction: either all are intuitive or all are acquired.
3
Verify that the consequent is false (batil)
The proof relies on the independent falsity of the consequent. For example, the concept of 'intellect' is acquired (kasbi) because one does not have an innate concept of intellect, while the concept of 'heat' is intuitive (daruri). Similarly, the assent 'the world is created' is acquired through reasoning, while the law of non-contradiction is intuitive. Thus, both extremes are false: not all are merely intuitive, nor all are merely acquired.
The falsity is established by counterexamples from experience.
4
Apply modus tollens to conclude the falsity of the antecedent
Since the consequent is false and the conditional is true (if antecedent then consequent), the antecedent must be false. Therefore, the negation of the antecedent is true: 'tasawwur and tasdiq are divided into necessary and acquired.'
This is a valid conditional inference, known as dalil al-khalf (proof by contradiction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما كانَ تقسيمُ التَّصورِ والتَّصديق إلى : الضَّروريّ والكسبي ضروريا ؛ لأنَّهما لو لم ينقسما إليهما ؛ لكانَ الجميعُ : إِمَّا بديهيا ، أو كسبيا والتّالي باطل بقسميه، فكذا المقدَّم. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : ضَرُوْرِيَّا) أي: بديهاً . (قَوْلُهُ: لَوْ لَمْ ... إلخ) مُقدَّم ؛ لَكَانَ الجميعُ ... إلخ تالي، وقوله: (لَكَانَ الجميع؛ أي: جميع أفرادِ التَّصور، وجميع أفراد التصديق، وقوله: (إما بديهيا)؛ أي : فقط ، (وإمَّا كسبيا ) فقط . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالتَّالِي) أي : وهو كونُ الجميع، إما بديهي أو كسبي. (قَوْلُهُ : فَكَذَا الْمُقَدَّمُ) أي : فالمقدَّمُ وهو عدم انقسام كُلِّ مِنَ التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ إلى الضَّروريّ والكسبي مثل التّالي في البطلان ؛ لأنَّ بطَلانَ اللازم يستلزم بطلانَ الملزوم؛ أي: وإذا بطل المقدَّمُ ثبت نقيضُه، وهو انقسامُ كُلِّ من التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ إلى الضّروريّ والكسبي، وهو المطلوب.
And indeed the division of tasawwur and tasdiq into necessary and acquired is necessary because if they were not divided into them, then all [individuals] would be either intuitive or acquired. And the consequent is false in both its divisions, so likewise the antecedent. Dassuqi: His saying 'necessary' i.e. intuitive. His saying 'if not... etc.' is the antecedent; 'then all... etc.' is the consequent; and his saying 'then all' i.e. all individuals of tasawwur and all individuals of tasdiq; and his saying 'either intuitive' i.e. only, 'or acquired' i.e. only. His saying 'and the consequent' i.e. that all are either intuitive or acquired. His saying 'so likewise the antecedent' i.e. the antecedent, which is the non-division of each of tasawwur and tasdiq into necessary and acquired, is like the consequent in falsity because the falsity of the necessary entails the falsity of the necessitating; i.e. when the antecedent is falsified, its opposite is established, which is the division of each of tasawwur and tasdiq into necessary and acquired, and that is the desired.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (dalil al-khalf) demonstrating the necessity of dividing tasawwur (concepts) and tasdiq (assents) into daruri (necessary/intuitive) and kasbi (acquired). The argument is a conditional syllogism: If they were not so divided (antecedent), then all individuals of each category would be either wholly intuitive or wholly acquired (consequent). This consequent is false because, as previously shown, some concepts (e.g., 'intellect') require acquisition, while others (e.g., 'heat') are intuitive; similarly, some assents (e.g., 'the world is created') are acquired, while the law of non-contradiction is intuitive. Since the consequent is false, the antecedent is also false, proving the division. Dassuqi's commentary clarifies each component: the antecedent is the negation of division, the consequent is the exhaustive dichotomy, and the refutation uses the falsity of both extremes. This proof is foundational for the epistemological framework of logic, ensuring that knowledge is not monolithic but admits both necessary and acquired components.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
ضروري
كسبي
دليل الخلف

Key Takeaway

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
