Error in Conceptions: A Critical Refinement
Debates whether error can occur in conceptual knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate on whether conceptions can be false and explain the distinction between knowledge of the aspect and knowledge of the thing.

Core Idea
Conceptions are always true; error is in the judgment linking them to reality.

This passage presents a critical discussion on the nature of error in knowledge. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif argues that conception (tasawwur) is never false because it always corresponds to what it is a conception of. However, al-Khiyali and Abd al-Hakim refine this by introducing a crucial distinction: the difference between knowledge of the aspect (al-‘ilm bi’l-wajh) and knowledge of the thing from that aspect (al-‘ilm bi’l-shay’ min dhalika’l-wajh). Understanding this distinction allows us to see how error can indeed occur in conceptual knowledge without violating the basic truth of conception itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The passage begins by noting that the previous commentary suggests error occurs in both conceptions and assents. Al-Sayyid is then quoted to argue the opposite: conception is never false.
2
Extract al-Sayyid's argument
Al-Sayyid provides an example: seeing a stone from afar and forming an image of a human. The image is a correct conception of 'human'; error is in the judgment that this image belongs to the stone.
3
Find al-Khiyali's objection
Al-Khiyali objects that al-Sayyid distinguishes between knowledge of the aspect (the image of the human) and knowledge of the thing from that aspect (the stone known through that image). The conception in the example is actually of the stone, not of the human.
4
Understand Abd al-Hakim's elaboration
Abd al-Hakim explains that if knowledge of the aspect were identical to knowledge of the thing from that aspect, then there would be no error. But they are distinct: the aspect is known truly, but the thing known through that aspect may not correspond.
5
Summarize the final position
The refined view is that error can occur in conception when we consider the conception as knowledge of the thing from an aspect, even though the conception of the aspect itself is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ إنَّ هذا الكلامَ مُشعِرٌ بوقوع الخطأ في التصوراتِ والتصديقات، وقالَ السَّيِّدُ في شَرح المواقف : لا يوصَفُ التَّصَوُّرُ بِعَدَمِ المطابقة أصلاً، فإنَّا إذا رأينا من بعيدٍ شَبَحاً هو حَجَرٌ مَثَلاً ، وحصلَ منهُ في أذهاننا صُورةُ إنسانٍ؛ فَتلكَ الصُّورة صورة للإنسانِ وعِلْمٌ تصوريٌّ بِهِ، والخطأُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي حُكْمِ العَقْلِ بأنَّ هذهِ الصُّورة للشَّبح المرئيّ، فالتَّصوراتُ كُلُّهَا مُطَابقةٌ لِمَا هِيَ تَصوُّرات له موجوداً كان أو معدوماً ؛ مُمكِنَاً كانَ أو مُمتَنِعاً، وعدم المطابقة في أحكام العقل المقارنةِ لِتلكَ التَّصوراتِ .ا.هـ. ، قال الخيالي في حاشية العقائد : هذا هو المشهور بين الجمهور، ويرد عليهِ أنَّهُ فرَّقَ بينَ العِلْمِ بالوجه والعِلْمِ بالشَّيءِ مِن ذَلِكَ الوجه ؛ فالمتصوَّرُ في المثال المذكور هو الشَّبحُ والصَّورةُ آلَةٌ لِمُلاحظتِهِ ا . هـ . . قال عبد الحكيم في حواشيهِ عليهِ حاصِلُهُ أَنَّ كَوْنَ تلكَ الصُّورةِ تَصوُّرٌ، وإدراكُ الإنسانِ موقوفٌ على أن يكونَ العِلْمُ بالوجه عينَ العِلْم بالشَّيء مِن ذلكَ الوجهِ؛ حتَّى يكونَ العِلْمُ بالشَّبَح مِن وجه الإنسانيَّةِ عَينَ العِلْمِ بالإنسانِ الَّذي هو وجهه، لكن الفرق ثابت، فإِنَّ مَعنى العِلْمِ بالوجه هو أنْ يحصل في الذهنِ صورةٌ تكونُ آلةٌ لملاحظةِ ذلكَ الوجه، فالوجه معلوم، والحاصل في الذهن صورته، ومعنى العِلْمِ بالشَّيء من ذلك الوجهِ أنْ يكونَ ذلك الوجه آلةٌ لِمُلاحظتِهِ، فالحاصل الذهن نفَسُ ذلكَ الوجه، والمعلوم بواسطتها ؛ ذلكَ الشَّيءُ، فالعِلْمُ بالوجه في المثالِ المذكور ؛ أعني العِلم بالإنسان وإنْ كانَ مُطَابِقاً ، لكنَّ العِلْمَ بالشَّيء من ذلكَ الوجهِ ليسَ مُطابِقاً، والمقصود في المثال المذكور هو هذا ؛ إذ المتصوَّرُ هو الشَّبَحُ، والصُّورة الإنسانيَّةُ آلةٌ لِمُلاحظته .ا.هـ. ، وفي المحشّي هنا كلامٌ لا معنى له . وهو عدم مطابقةِ النِّسبة الكلاميَّة للخارج؛ أي: إِنَّ هذهِ النِّسبة المستفادةَ مِنَ الكلام هي في نفْسِهَا ليست كذلك، فيرجع للكذب، وهذا ظاهر في التّصديقاتِ، وأمَّا في التّصوراتِ ؛ فقد تقدَّمَ لَكَ ما فيها، هذا معنى ما في في المحشّي أنَّ الخطأ هوَ كَالصَّوابِ ؛ يكون صفةٌ للحُكّم ، ومعناهما غيرُ المطابق للواقع والمطابق له، وقد يكونان صفةٌ للفعل، ومعناهما غير الموافق للغرض والموافق
Then this statement indicates the occurrence of error in conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). Al-Sayyid [al-Sharif] said in his commentary on al-Mawaqif: 'Conception (tasawwur) is never described as lacking correspondence (mutabaqa) at all. For if we see from a distance a figure (shabah) that is, for example, a stone, and from it there occurs in our minds an image of a human, then that image is an image of a human and a conceptual knowledge of it; the error is only in the judgment of the mind that this image belongs to the visible figure. Therefore, all conceptualizations correspond to what they are conceptualizations of, whether the object exists or does not exist, whether it is possible or impossible. Lack of correspondence occurs in the judgments of the mind that accompany those conceptualizations.' End quote. Al-Khiyali said in his gloss on al-Aqa'id: 'This is the well-known view among the majority, but it is objected that he distinguished between knowledge of the aspect (al-wajh) and knowledge of the thing from that aspect. So the conceptualized thing in the mentioned example is the figure, and the image is a tool for observing it.' End quote. Abd al-Hakim said in his gloss on it: 'The gist is that this image being a conception and the perception of the human depends on knowledge of the aspect being the same as knowledge of the thing from that aspect, so that knowledge of the figure from the aspect of humanity is the same as knowledge of the human which is its aspect. But the difference is established. For the meaning of knowledge of the aspect is that an image occurs in the mind which is a tool for observing that aspect; so the aspect is known, and what occurs in the mind is its image. The meaning of knowledge of the thing from that aspect is that that aspect becomes a tool for observing the thing; so what occurs in the mind is the very aspect, and the thing known through it is that thing. Therefore, knowledge of the aspect in the mentioned example – i.e., knowledge of the human – even if it corresponds, yet knowledge of the thing from that aspect does not correspond. And the intended point in the mentioned example is this, for the conceptualized thing is the figure, and the human image is a tool for observing it.' End quote. In the glossator (al-muhashshi) here there is meaningless speech. It is the lack of correspondence of the propositional relation (al-nisba al-kalamiyya) to the external; i.e., this relation obtained from speech is in itself not so, so it reduces to falsehood (kadhib). This is apparent in assents (tasdiqat). As for conceptualizations, what is in them has already been presented to you. This is the meaning of what is in the glossator: that error (khata') is like correctness (sawab); they are attributes of judgment (hukm), meaning non-corresponding and corresponding to reality. And they may be attributes of action, meaning non-conforming and conforming to the purpose.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion on whether error can occur in conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). It begins by noting that the previous commentary suggests error applies to both tasawwur and tasdiq. Then al-Sayyid al-Sharif argues that tasawwur is never false because conception is simply the mental image of a thing; it always corresponds to what it is a conception of, even if the thing does not exist. Error only enters through judgment (hukm) when the mind incorrectly relates the image to an external object. For example, seeing a stone from afar and forming the image of a human: the image of a human is a correct conception of 'human' (it matches the concept of human), but the error is in the judgment that this image is the image of the visible stone. Al-Khiyali objects by distinguishing between knowledge of the aspect (al-‘ilm bi’l-wajh) and knowledge of the thing from that aspect (al-‘ilm bi’l-shay’ min dhalika’l-wajh). In the example, the aspect is 'human' which is correctly known, but the thing (the stone) is not correctly known from that aspect; the conception of the stone is erroneous because it is actually an image of a human, not a stone. Abd al-Hakim elaborates that if knowledge of the aspect were identical to knowledge of the thing from that aspect, then there would be no error; but since they are distinct, error can occur in the latter even if the former is correct. The passage ends with a clarification that in tasdiqat, error is clearly about false propositions, while in tasawwurat the issue is more subtle. For instance, in Islamic law, the concept of 'riba' is correctly understood; error occurs if one judges a particular transaction to be riba when it is not. Similarly, the conception of 'angel' is true regardless of existence; error is in judging that a particular being is an angel.

Key Terms
خطأ
تصور
تصديق
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sayyid al-Sharif, what is the locus of error in the example of seeing a stone and forming an image of a human?
A) The image of the human is false
B) The judgment that the image corresponds to the stone
C) The conception of 'human' is false
D) The act of perception is erroneous

Key Takeaway
Conceptions are always true as representations; error occurs only in judgments that link them to external objects or in confusing the aspect with the thing itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following: In fiqh, one forms a conception of 'riba'. If a person sees a transaction and conceptualizes it as 'riba' based on a superficial similarity, is the conception itself erroneous or the judgment? Use the distinction from this lesson to analyze.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Means to Conceptual and Assenetic Ends
Author classifies near and far means to tasawwuri and tasdiqi ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the means to conceptual and assenetic knowledge as near, far, or more distant, and identify the logical tools involved.

Core Idea
Knowledge tools are arranged in a hierarchy: some give direct access, others require composition.

In logic, the path to knowledge is not one-size-fits-all. Some tools yield results directly, while others require assembly. Al-Dusuqi's commentary lays out this hierarchy for both conceptual knowledge (understanding what a thing is) and assenetic knowledge (judging that something is true). By mastering this classification, you will see how definitions, propositions, and syllogisms work together in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two desired ends
Notice that al-Dusuqi distinguishes between 'al-matlub al-tasawwuri' (the conceptual desired) and 'al-matlub al-tasdiqi' (the assenetic desired). The goal is to reach each type of knowledge.
Conceptual knowledge is about essences; assenetic knowledge is about truth.
2
Find the near and far means for conceptual ends
For the conceptual desired, the near means is 'al-qawl al-sharih' (the explanatory statement, i.e., definition). The far means is 'al-kulliyyat al-khams' (the five universals), which must be composed into a definition.
The five universals are genus, species, difference, property, and common accident.
3
Find the near, far, and more distant means for assenetic ends
For the assenetic desired, the near means is 'al-qiyas' (the syllogism), the far means is 'al-qadiyya' (the proposition), and a more distant means is a conceptual known used as subject or predicate. Each step requires composition: subject/predicate → proposition → syllogism.
The more distant means (ab'ad) is the farthest, requiring two compositions.
4
Understand the footnote by al-Sharnubi
The footnote clarifies that 'the five universals' here means only some of them, excluding common accident ('arad 'amm) and species (naw'). This is because definition uses genus, difference, and property; species and common accident are not used in complete definitions.
This refinement is important for applying the five universals correctly in defining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي واعلم أن المعلوم التصوري الموصل للمطلوب التصوري قريب وهو القول الشارح وبعيد وهو الكليات الخمس (۱) وذلك لأن القول الشارح يوصل للمطلوب التصوري مباشرة والكليات الخمس توصل إليه بواسطة تركيب القول الشارح منها وأن المعلوم التصديقي الموصل للمطلوب التصديقي قريب كالقياس وبعيد كالقضية لأن القياس موصل للمطلوب مباشرة والقضية موصلة إليه بواسطة تركيب القياس منها وقد يوصل المعلوم التصوري إلى التصديقي لكن إيصالاً أبعد ككونه موضوعاً أو محمولاً فإن كلا منهما يوصل للمطلوب التصديقي بواسطة تركيب القضية منهما الموصلة بواسطة تركيب القياس منها الموصل للمطلوب مباشرة فتحصل أن الموصل للمطلوب التصوري إما قريب أو بعيد والموصل للمطلوب التصديقي إما قريب أو بعيد أو أبعد. (۱) (قوله والكليات الخمس أي بعضها فإنهم لم يعتبروا التعريف بالعرض العام ولا بالنوع كما يأتي. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.)
Al-Dusuqi said: And know that the conceptual known which leads to the conceptual desired is near, and it is the explanatory statement, and far, and it is the five universals. That is because the explanatory statement leads to the conceptual desired directly, and the five universals lead to it by way of composing the explanatory statement from them. And that the assenetic known which leads to the assenetic desired is near, like the syllogism, and far, like the proposition, because the syllogism leads to the desired directly, and the proposition leads to it by way of composing the syllogism from it. And sometimes the conceptual known leads to the assenetic, but more distantly, like being a subject or a predicate, for each of them leads to the assenetic desired by way of composing the proposition from them, which leads by way of composing the syllogism from it, which leads to the desired directly. So it follows that the means to the conceptual desired is either near or far, and the means to the assenetic desired is either near or far, or more distant. (1) (His statement: 'and the five universals' meaning some of them, for they did not consider definition by common accident or by species as will come. End quote from al-Sharnubi.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the means (muwasil) to knowledge, as presented by al-Dusuqi in his commentary. It distinguishes between conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) and assenetic knowledge (tasdiq). For a conceptual desired end (matlub tasawwuri), the near means is the explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih), which is the definition (had or rasm), while the far means is the five universals (al-kulliyyat al-khams: genus, species, difference, property, common accident). The five universals are far because they must be composed into a definition to directly give conceptual knowledge. For an assenetic desired end (matlub tasdiqi), the near means is the syllogism (qiyas), which directly produces certainty, and the far means is the proposition (qadiyya), which must be combined into a syllogism. Additionally, the author notes that a conceptual known (like a subject or predicate) can lead to an assenetic conclusion in a more distant way (ab'ad), because it must first be composed into a proposition, then into a syllogism. The passage concludes with a summary: the means to the conceptual is either near or far, and the means to the assenetic is either near, far, or more distant. The footnote by al-Sharnubi clarifies that the five universals here refer only to some of them, excluding common accident and species. This hierarchy is fundamental in logic for understanding how definitions and arguments serve as tools for knowledge.

Key Terms
المعلوم التصوري
المعلوم التصديقي
الموصل
القول الشارح
الكليات الخمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the near means to the assenetic desired?
A) The explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih)
B) The syllogism (al-qiyas)
C) The proposition (al-qadiyya)
D) The five universals

Key Takeaway
The means to knowledge are not all equal: definitions and syllogisms are direct, while universals and propositions require composition, and subject/predicate terms are the most indirect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' Identify which parts are near and far means for the assenetic desired. Explain what would be missing if only the proposition 'every intoxicant is forbidden' were used.
?
Classify the following as near, far, or more distant means: (a) the definition 'wine is an intoxicating beverage', (b) the predicate 'intoxicant', (c) the syllogism that nabidh is forbidden.
